      \nRyan, I feel fairly sure you're reading Raffaele in bad faith here.
      But let me propose a few situations. I'd like to know in which of these, if any, would you consider denying a request for such to be "petty and selfish." I'll stick with game text examples, since we are, after all, on an gaming forum. And let's assume, in all cases, that I've acquired my original copy of the game text legally.

      1) I want to reproduce parts of the text in a new document for reference in play. For the GM or players. Maybe new characters sheets, or rules cheat sheets.
      2) I want to reproduce parts of the text in a new document, as above, but I also want to share them with strangers on the internet because I think my documents are useful or cool or would otherwise enhance others enjoyment of the game.
      3) I only have a PDF of the game text and I want to print out large chunks of it to use as a reference at the table, perhaps because I lack a laptop or e-reader
      4) I only have a PDF of the game text and friends I've played the game with or told the game about are interested in reading it more thoroughly, so I want to send them a copy
      5) I want to hack the game. Maybe I want to use its setting with another system, or maybe I want to take apart its system to use for a different purpose.
      6) I want to do some kind of art project / collage with the game. I want to take bits of it, rip them out, and mash them together with chunks from Wraeththu , Nobilis, and passages from the Bible.

      Posted By: fnord3125But so do I. And suppose for the moment that I wish to express myself by juxtaposing your text with the text of a KKK pamphlet.

      Do you believe you have the right to deny me that mode of expression?
      \nSure, in some cases. People's rights bang into one another all the time.

      In this example, you took my expression and took some KKK group's expression, and juxtaposed them. If you want to post that as art or political speech, I would wince and suck it up, because your artistic freedom and your political freedom trump my freedom of expression most of the time. If you want to do it for some other reason, like to damage my business reputation, I'd assert my moral rights over the work to stop you. Intent does matter in the law.
      \n
      Posted By: cwilburWe're still arguing from different premises. I don't believe in copyright as a natural right; I believe in it as a way of funding the creation of useful and beautiful works. As long as it's serving that goal, it's a good thing. When it stops serving that goal, it's a bad thing.
      \nWell, there's no use in us arguing if we can't agree on the premise. In general, copyright law (and rights, as recognized by governments) exists to encourage--not fund--the creation of useful and beautiful works. It's an important distinction. Funding is one kind of encouragement. Faith that one's works cannot be subverted or stolen is another. Copyright is a whole bundle of rights, and the right to derive income from one's work is just a small part of them. If you want to maintain such a narrow view of copyright, that's fine, but there's little use in you and I continuing this discussion.
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Posted By: fnord3125I'm... on the fence about this assertion. You don't think they ever do it because they want to support the creator? I will admit that I don't think I've ever bought anything that I've pirated, even from artists I really like. I HAVE, however, definitely bought things that I already had acquired or could easily acquire for free. At least a few times I've bought thingsspecificallybecause the author was cool enough togivethem away for free (Cory Doctorow novels, Jonathan Coulton music, and the Eclipse Phase RPG, for example).
      \nThat's why I stuck that "as a matter of principle" qualifier into that sentence, actually: while pirates pay authors for many reasons, the only principled reason I've encountered is the one where you pay to make up for having pirated the thing. That's a guilty motivation. There's also other motivations in play when people pay creators, such as becoming really fannish about it and wanting to support the starving artist, but that's not a principle, that's just motivation. When a pirate wants to justify himself, he'll often bring in the idea that he's not "really" causing any financial loss because he'll buy the things that deserve it. That's a principle (applying to his actions in general), and it's also guilty thinking, as the pirate realizes that piracy isn't maintainable as a general principle of the culture industry economy without the money coming from somewhere. So that's where we get this pretty common current justification for piracy, "I only pirate to check out things, and I pay for the ones that truly deserve it in hindsight."

      For what it's worth, I'm not personally convinced by the rationalization where I'm just "checking out" a product prior to purchase. The intrigue of the unknown is a major part of the marketing strategy for many of these things, so it's ludicrous to say that it's somehow OK for me to satisfy my curiousity and then only make the purchase if I want long-term ownership of the piece. That's a very arbitrary way of redefining the nature of the product which the author might have made specifically to work with that initial curiousity instead of striving for a permanently intriguing thing. Even if you're just watching that movie once to see if it's worthwhile to buy, you're still very much consuming the product and removing a significant part of the motivation the publisher relies on in convincing you to make the buy. Perhaps this has some slight, very slight credence in roleplaying due to how important a long-term relationship with a game is for a person to truly experience it, but nobody probably would deny that curiousity is still a significant market force in roleplaying as well.

      The above might seem contradictory considering that I'm pro-revision on copyright matters, but I'm just trying to see things as they are, not to build an ideological fantasy. I think that the current state of things where piracy has to be justified by elevating the pirate himself into an unilateral judge of who gets paid and who doesn't is pretty screwy and has only evolved to make pirates feel good about themselves. It'd be better if the moral pressure was taken away, allowing people to dispense with this "false consciousness" (heh) and just get on with their cultural interactions. That requires creators developing new methods of commerce, though, something that does not rely on controlling the work's distribution.

      Then again, I don't know - perhaps moral pressure is effective as a market device? Some creators seem to report that they get more money out of the piracy machine than it takes by various metrics, and much of that seems to be guilt-money that would likely not be there if the creator gave the work away unfettered by demands of recompense. Perhaps it benefits creators to encourage the development of this "good pirate" archetype where people consider it their moral responsibility to remove the files after viewing unless they like it enough to buy it, or however this attitude might be best expressed. Many financial systems in the world (most of them charities) work almost purely by moral means, after all.

      Posted By: Eero TuovinenStill, think of it - whatisthe difference between a public library and the Internet, and why should one have the right to exist and function while the other doesn't?
      \nNo one is saying that the Internet does have the right to exist and function. It's a medium. It exists. People use it for good and bad. I'm not calling for shutting down the Internet, for god's sake. I am calling for people to shame certain behavior that was illegal and (I believe) immoral (in a small way--I'm not comparing it to murder, people) before the advent of file sharing. Shame it, or at the very least stop supporting it.

      Your public library analogy has flaws I could drive a Lamborghini through. Or a Lancia Stratos. Libraries buy their books. As delegates of the government, of the people, they pay the author and then share the books with a reasonably limited number of people in a limited way (two weeks, or only on the premises in the case of ebooks). I don't think these limits are ridiculous at all. These limits preserve the author's copyright as much as possible. In other words, the library is carefully exercising Fair Use. If a library started printing and binding copies of my PDF and giving them away to thousands of patrons, I'd think they'd overstepped the bounds of Fair Use.

      \n

      What you're talking about, Adam, is a na\xefve view of how publication works, from the standpoint of someone who doesn't work creatively. That's not an insult, but it is criticism. It means that you're operating by looking at the last 1% of the tip of an iceberg.

      Posted By: guildofbladesOk, lets reframe this slightly. Copyright violation is against the law...hence why there is a law. So someone breaking copyright law is a criminal.       Posted By: guildofbladesCopyright violation is against the law...hence why there is a law. So someone breaking copyright law is a criminal.

      \n As far as I can understand Charlton's argument, and Eero's, it is this:

      1. Creators have no inherit rights surrounding works they create.
      2. Copyright is a privilege granted to creators by governments to encourage them to create things.
      3. That privilege is oft superseded by the public's right to possess creative works...
      4. ...such that ordinary citizens should have free access to these works when they can be copied freely as in the case of electronic music and documents.
      5. Not just copied freely, but extended, changed, manipulated, and so on.
      6. Not just copied freely, but copied into any media and redistributed to the world.
      7. People can copy however they like, without permission of the author or compensation to the author.

      To extend #1, Rafu and Charlton seem to believe that creators have a responsibility to use their gifts to create works that they give to the public, free of charge.

      \n For what it's worth, I agree with everybody who's said that many pirate behaviours are illegal. This seems non-contentious to me despite a certain grey area as regards private copying and distribution (allowed by the Finnish law, for example), as those grey areas certainly don't apply to the large-scale distribution networks that exist today internationally.

      This part of the discussion is so utterly obvious that I don't really have anything more to say about it. The real conundrum with piracy is not whether it's illegal, but whether it should be. That's a different order of issue, and one where we can't rely on the law or precedent to lead the way. If we justify the existence of anti-piracy laws with the fact that there are anti-piracy laws, then our legal system is now and forevermore in stasis: no law can ever be changed, for the existence of that law justifies its own existence. That's textbook circular reasoning.

      The real issue we're discussing, it seems to me, is whether copyright is moral and practical. Adam has been arguing that it is moral while I've been arguing that while it might be moral, it's not an absolute moral issue, and may therefore be set aside for the good of all when it becomes impractical to enforce without undue compromises in other areas of life. In reference to Joshua, I wouldn't characterize this discussion as a debate so much as a conversation, we've been telling each other how we think about this matter. Adam's point, as I understand it, is that he has a strong moral intuition about copyright being right, so much so that he finds it mind-boggling that we don't share this conviction. I've been sort of trying to cast doubt upon this intuition with a variety of rhetorics because to me it seems like an entrenched belief that has no basis once you question it thoroughly, but well, we know how well that sort of thing works in real life

      I do agree with Ryan's insight that it shows poor form for society to act in bad faith regarding creators who published their work imagining that they might benefit off it instead of having the work be pirated every which way. They were betrayed by their own ignorance and the society around them, which mislead them into thinking that their work would be protected, when in fact it is not. I wrote about this earlier in the thread: the real tragedy of piracy is that creators have been and still are utterly unprepared for the reality of digital distribution, which causes anger and shame and a general questioning of the moral nature of the universe. It feels a lot like theft or a similar violation, I'm sure. It's a further tragedy when the myth of copyright is deliberately upheld to school new creators into believing in rights that exist only on paper, and it becomes even more tragic when people start following the idea that copyright needs to be saved, leading into actual deeds of aggression towards the pirate networks in a futile attempt to change the world back into the way it was. (I'm using the word "futile" deliberately here: so far all solutions for the piracy problem have been utterly ineffective, while the potentially effective hypothetical solutions have been awful and authoritarian. This is the basis for my stance on this matter.)

      Posted By: Adam DrayAs far as I can understand Charlton's argument, and Eero's, it is this:

      1. Creators have no inherit rights surrounding works they create.
      2. Copyright is a privilege granted to creators by governments to encourage them to create things.
      3. That privilege is oft superseded by the public's right to possess creative works...
      4. ...such that ordinary citizens should have free access to these works when they can be copied freely as in the case of electronic music and documents.
      5. Not just copied freely, but extended, changed, manipulated, and so on.
      6. Not just copied freely, but copied into any media and redistributed to the world.
      7. People can copy however they like, without permission of the author or compensation to the author.
      \nAlmost there. I'd correct points from #3 forward to clarify that I do not see any public right to possession of creative works as a matter of natural right, but only a practical situation that makes it impossible to distribute anything without using free distribution models or having somebody else do that for you. This in turn implies that points 4-7 are not moral dictums for me, but practical statements of fact: those are in reality things people can do, and there's little that we should be doing to stop them, because all cures I've seen proposed are worse than the disease. Therefore it's better to catch the wave and adapt to the situation, as it's only going to get worse for old-style copyright industry: more people will become habitual pirates, what anti-piracy measures are tried will be more routinely cracked and any legal cases will only become more spectacular, unjust and controversial when people are dragged from their homes to answer for crimes that all neighbours very well know to be theirs as well.

      So it's not "should have" as you say in #4, but "do factually have". The only moral component of my argument is that unless somebody can point to a method of piracy-prevention that doesn't infringe on traditional personal freedoms of speech and privacy, and the freedom of the Internet, then I can't in good conscience support copyright in its modern form any longer. A system that only punishes the law-abiding by having them shoulder the entire financial burden of the culture industry is not going to last long.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\ncwilbur \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Not quite, at least in my case, Adam - you're rather more extreme than I am.

      First, the ethical argument.

      1. Creators do not have the inherent right to control distribution of their work. They do have the right to be recognized as creator of the work, and to designate authoritative versions of their work.
      2. (same) Copyright is a privilege granted to creators by governments to encourage them to create things.
      3. That privilege is balanced against the public's right to possess creative works.
      4. In exchange for that privilege of monopoly protection, creators have a duty to make their work available for a reasonable price.
      5. When the creator is no longer making the work available for a reasonable price -- such as when a book goes out of print -- then the privilege of monopoly protection no longer applies.
      6. Extending, changing, manipulating, copied into any medium, all fine -- as long as the contributions are recognized.
      7. People can copy however they like, but reasonable compensation, preferably in line with what the author requests, is ethically required.
      8. All of this applies to *creators* -- not warehouse staff, middlemen, publishers, whatever.

      Now, the pragmatic argument.

      1. Technology makes it possible and easy for people to do things that are not ethical, even by the standards I outlined above.
      2. Enacting laws to work against the progress of technology is a fool's game.
      3. The same technological changes that make unauthorized copying easy make authorized copying easy.
      4. In the long term, creators who work with technology rather than against it will benefit.
      5. In the long run, middlemen who do not add value will cease to exist.

      I mean, to take an example: it is possible for someone, right this minute, to send $18 to Vincent Baker, and within a day, have a shiny PDF of Apocalypse World in my hands -- and the marginal cost of that PDF to him is measured in pennies. I have no idea what PayPal charges him for fees, so we'll say he makes $15 in profit from that sale. What if things were like they were twenty years ago? Well, suppose I wanted to buy his book for $28, which is what he charges now for print+pdf. $14 of that would have gone to the store; $7 to the warehouse and distributor. $2-3 to produce the book leaves him with a profit of $4-$5.

      So, in terms of remuneration to the creator, he's doing a *lot* better than he was before. And if I give the PDF to two other people, he's breaking even. Now, if I give the PDF to two other people who have ethics similar to myself, and they like it, that's two more sales for him that wouldn't have happened if I hadn't given out the PDF. (As an example, I know that this has happened with Remember Tomorrow and In A Wicked Age: I gave the PDF to people who liked it and subsequently bought it.)

      Now, in this example, who loses out most by the march of technology? Well, there's no retailer taking in $14 based on someone else's work, and no warehouse/distributor middleman making $7 based on someone else's work. Who stands to gain the most by fighting to preserve the traditional publishing model instead of adapting to new technology?
      \n

      Posted By: Joshua A.C. NewmanWhat you're talking about, Adam, is a na\xefve view of how publication works, from the standpoint of someone who doesn't work creatively. That's not an insult, but itiscriticism. It means that you're operating by looking at the last 1% of the tip of an iceberg.
      \nYour points are all valid. What I'm describing isn't reality just a suggested way of selling creative products. It's a counterpoint to today's publishing model and copyright legislation and thus could only work if the majority or at least an easily visible minority of publishing was made that way.

      As you have noted, it doesn't work just yet, and I'm not encouraging anyone who's buying food from selling games to try making their money this way. However, I'm positive there will be people who will try to make this work. Who knows, they might even succeed. Kickstarter is a baby-step in that direction, and I'm anticipating more steps following the same path.

      Posted By: Adam DrayI've backed my "intuition" with a reasoned argument that moral copyright is an extension of Freedom of Expression. You never addressed the original post where I made that argument, nor the follow-up posts where I referred back to it.
      \nI thought I did - this was the part with the Ku Klux Klan, right? Remove an author's copyright and you also remove any audience expectation that the author would control it, obviously enough. This is not to say that it's not possible to paint a creator in negative light once creator rights are relaxed, but that seems to me like a price that has to be paid, again - it's not convincing me that the creator's right to have his work remain pristine is more important than the ability people have today to publish things anonymously and without censorship. Those two rights are polar opposites in the digital world, as the only way to enforce Adam's right to not get associated with Ku Klux Klan is to establish effective controls that consistently prevent people from publishing such tripe.

      But yes, you're right in that you have certainly argued for your position. I didn't want to imply that you didn't, I just summarized that the basis of your argument is that you find creator rights so very valuable that they should not be given up in the face of the practical difficulties. Seems like that's in the center of this.

      In any case, no barbs were intended, and it's nothing personal to you that rhetorics don't convince people. I have fun trying to explain the way I see this issue in various ways (that virginity comparison is still gold), but it remains a fact that people don't change their opinions on worldview stuff like this on the strength of some Internet rhetoric. We'll have to give it time and look at the matter fresh, and perhaps one of us finds later on that he's actually convinced by something that is being said in this thread.

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I'm a writer and have been a principal in a startup videogame company that had our product--made with many hours of work plus thousands of dollars of investment--taken by our distributor and sold outside the agreed-upon territory. They didn't pay us for what they sold in the agreed-upon territory either, but we were definitely harmed to the tune of over a million dollars, and the company perished. We got a judgment of $1.6 million dollars but didn't have the money to enforce it. Bottom line is, this had real consequences for us, our families, and our investors. I saw not a dime for eighteen months of work.

      Fiction writers now are very uneasy about eBook proliferation. The question ultimately is, how much of the revenue will find its way back to the author? We don't have the musician's option of performing concerts or shows. Patronage might be an answer, but my experience is that most people who download files for free don't actually care to pay. Sorry, but my personal experience with friends who live on BitTorrent is that they pretty much just don't want to pay for anything. While that may be a natural impulse, it's still unethical. We're not talking about grabbing copies of OOP material, which is in my mind the only justification that approaches the ethical.

      Doctorow may be making enough to live off his writing (I don't know if he actually is), but I don't know any other writers seeing enough sales from eBooks to support them as writers. The folks I'm talking to--widely published and making a full-time living today off dead tree publication--aren't reporting electronic sales as anything more than a blip, not even a promising trend. I'm highly skeptical that living on the long tail will enable more than a tiny percentage of writers to earn nontrivial income.

      I'm reading a lot of high-minded, cogent arguments about our brave new digital world, and though from a practical standpoint I agree the genie is out of the bottle and we'll have to adapt as a practical matter. On the other hand, I think these claims ignore the very real consequences piracy can have on creators. It may be we're headed for a world where all art becomes a hobby, except where the creator is employed by a corporation financially able to leverage a mass-distribution and marketing channel. I think there's value in having creators able to make an independent living off their own work. I don't view this as entitlement. If I'm not going to be paid for my work, I won't put the massive effort out to create. I may do some personal stuff or submit to hobby press to scratch my creative itch, but that's it. If you're a small press hobby publisher, piracy may not hurt you much, since you know going in you're going to sell a small number of copies, and you're probably directing your effort toward a distinct demographic that will buy X number of copies anyway.

      For me, the notion that I could bust my ass for a year or two, sell my book on a royalty agreement, then have it copied and distributed without compensation is offensive. While it may not technically rise to the legal definition of theft, I nevertheless feel victimized. It feels like the moral equivalent of theft. Sure, it's not the same level of intrusion as violation of your home, but the creative work is your baby, and if it's copied without permission, it's essentially a theft of my time and effort. If I choose to release my work for free, that's one thing. If I don't, then an unauthorized and uncompensated copy constitutes, in my view, a parasitic taking. It ignores the labor that went into the book. It ignores the fact that the limit on copyright is part of the creator's choice to control the supply of the product that hits the market. It potentially impacts my income and my ability to feed my family using my hard-won professional skill. I can still sell my writing as a service, just not as a good. I suspect this is the future for professional creative writing.

      If electronic copies are OK across the board, what about taking a book and making a movie out of it? How should copyright apply in that instance? Does an author have copyright in one instance but not the other? Note that I'm not talking about copies for personal use. I'm saying that a digital copy of a current for-sale work distributed without permission has a commercial impact by affecting the supply and demand for my work and is therefore a taking, the moral equivalent of theft. As I said, the only possible exception might be for OOP work that's no longer commercially available (I don't count inflated eBay offerings as "commercially available").
      \n

      Posted By: HexabolicThe folks I'm talking to--widely published and making a full-time living today off dead tree publication--aren't reporting electronic sales as anything more than a blip, not even a promising trend. I'm highly skeptical that living on the long tail will enable more than a tiny percentage of writers to earn nontrivial income.
      \nWriters making a living off dead tree publication are probably the most fucked-over people in the whole creative universe at this point; they get fucked by the publishers, by the bookstores, by the used book market, by public libraries (according to publishers, anyway), by the (perceived?) overall decline in the number of readers, and if eBook devices ever really become a significant force in the marketplace, they'll almost certainly end up getting fucked by file sharing.

      Sadly, I'm part of the problem. I read all the time, and have shelves all over my home packed so full of books that I have to keep some of them on the floor...but how many of these were purchased new, where at least some of the money might trickle back to the authors? I think I might have enough to fill up a backpack. Maybe. If it wasn't a very big backpack.

      As far as the professional writers out there might be concerned, I was a parasite and a thief even before the internet came into the picture, since I've been enjoying their work for decades without giving them even the tiniest bit of financial compensation for it. Hell, if I wanted to steal books from the internet at this point (and I don't, having neither Kindle nor Nook nor iPad, and I really hate reading things via PDF), it seems that I wouldn't be screwing over writers any more than I already do. I would be screwing over the local used bookstores, though, which would suck because I love them.

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n

      \n
      \n
      Posted By: HexabolicIf electronic copies are OK across the board, what about taking a book and making a movie out of it? How should copyright apply in that instance?
      \nThere's a world of difference between monitoring a movie studio and monitoring a hundred million anonymous Internet users. This is why I don't expect the commercial control facet of copyright to disappear - creators will probably retain their commercial monopoly on their own work in the future to the same degree that they do today, as it's ultimately relatively easy to track down any significant commercial activity, such as the making and distribution of a movie. The movie-maker can't make his movie anonymously and distribute it anonymously if he's to make money off it, so he's going to get caught just the same as ever.

      Of course even if you're the only one who has the legal right to sell your work and authorize spin-off products, free distribution will still kill the actual product dead as a market entity, at least compared to the current situation. Is the culture industry we have today bloated, or is it prosperous? Will we be better off when only one in a hundred of today's culture industry professionals work in this industry? Or will it be a dark age of civilization? Sure will be interesting to find out. On the one hand it's easy to imagine how dull life would be without 200 new Hollywood movies per year, but on the other hand we already have so much, culturally, that I'm a bit hesitant to claim that I need those 200 new movies every year to develop as a human being and enjoy my quality of life. There's already so much culture out there that I'll never be able to sample everything, so just measuring our prosperity by the amount of money poured into the culture industry seems wrong.

      We as creators have an immense self-interest in the copyright law, which makes us all somewhat suspect as commentators on the matter. It's like asking car dealers to do your city planning. Ultimately I see the inability to imagine a world without copyright as entitlement; perhaps it is justified if your work truly is so valuable and indispensable. The fact remains, though, that the rest of the society is not responsible for providing a single author a living wage, and when enforcing copyright becomes unfeasible, the society is no more responsible for saving the culture industry than it was for saving the horse coach business when cars were invented. We need to prove ourselves viable by our own actions, as it is untenable to ask the state to enforce conditions that make our old ways possible indefinitely. If we can't make our horse fly (to compete with that autocar from the last simile), then we deserve our whole field of endeavour to shrink into a cottage industry and a mere hobby. If new cultural work is truly requisited by society, the money for it will be there one way or another even with the omnipresent Internet. Sucks for us, but let's not get in the way of progress - that never ends well.

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n

      \n Eero, I don't disagree with the practical arguments you're advancing, and I appreciate your thoughtful, patient analysis. I'm not arguing society is responsible for offering authors or anyone else a living wage. I am saying that we're at a crux where choices about policy and law can have an impact. I think copyright is valuable as a commercial encouragement for creators, and I am skeptical that we should simply dispense with it. Even open source software operates under license constraints that can bring tangible consequences where violation is discovered. Enforcement may be difficult and even scattershot, but I think we can find a reasonable enforcement model that provides some protection to creators without automatically requiring resort to authoritarian means. Note that I'm not arguing for unlimited copyright. From a policy standpoint, I suppose my take has congruency to demands for fair trade or reasonable protectionism. Just because something is possible doesn't make it inevitable or optimal. It's a fair trade in some ways. If someone chooses to download an unauthorized file, he or she is presumably accepting the risk of being hit with draconian penalties. That risk constitutes a non-monetary price of sorts.

      Ultimately, the actual long-term answer will likely be writers riding the long tail, pushed along by viral marketing efforts. Publishers may find a niche here for helping produce and market certain works. I suspect quality will drop inasmuchas editors play a huge role in manuscript quality. Books will probably split between self-publishing sources like Lulu and limited hard-copy publications for a few heavily promoted books that fall into a best-seller category, much as the music industry puts out heavily promoted pop bands du jour. These will be pushed as artifacts ahead of ebook release. Bookstores that sell new books are likely to go the way of the dinosaur. Used bookstores will hang on longer, maybe a few decades. Most authors will probably follow the shareware model for works sold as goods. Aside from the few "pop band" discoveries, other creative writers will work off a hobby model, and I don't think the particular medium is as adaptable as music. There may be a small market for crafty authors who produce unique artifacts like handmade book covers, special paper, additional maps, etc.

      I view anti-piracy tech measures as reasonable to a point. They're much like car locks in that they're meant to keep out the casual, opportunistic thief, but not the pro. It's less about pursuing the illusion of absolute enforceability and more about putting up enough of a hurdle to discourage folks from casual copying. That said, this approach is doomed. There really isn't anything that can't be cracked absent Brobdingnagian measures that compromise the delivery of the work itself (by crushing it with encryption code, for example).

      Accounting for Taste, you're right that the publishing industry doesn't push much income at writers, at least mid-list or lower. You're also right in pointing out that the current model has shortcomings. The publishing industry has been in a reconfiguration for at least a decade, beset by competition with other media and increasing internet proliferation. Books are just not as popular. At the same time, I suspect the mid-list will vanish entirely in the new paradigm, and book quality generally will suffer. Producing books does actually take a lot of effort and cost. The model is pretty well tracked. I don't see the onrushing train of digital books pushing more income to writers, though. I think overall the opposite will happen. Many pro writers you see on the shelves are already putting out books around their day jobs. These folks might still write, but they sure won't make more.

      Libraries don't fuck them over, nor do used book stores. There's still a limit on supply. Libraries buy books, sometimes several copies of one book. Used bookstores traffick in previously sold copies. The continued presence of a transferable artifact usable by one person at a time isn't fucking anyone over. I don't think this argument touches the same issues as digital copying, and no author I'm aware of has a problem with used bookstores or libraries. In many ways, libraries are a great example of free distribution with some revenue going back to the writer.

      \n I have uninformed opinions on this subject that no one wants or needs to hear, but the peripheral topic of libraries as middle-men who may or may not be fucking over artists interests me.

      One of forty-two reasons libraries and librarians rock is that they sometimes act as filtering middle-men reviewers/critics/purveyors of quality above and beyond what publishers have decided to put an advertising budget behind. I love all the stories about small books, especially children or YA books that get a huge word-of-mouth bump from libraries. Books of quality and artistic merit that no one might have noticed had it not been for a librarian and a few of their professional associates (and a few of their professional associates, etc.) who found it and shared it via reading hours or similar. And then stocked it in quantity before the demand even warranted it.

      There are a lot of great and deserving books that might never would have gained a wide audience without libraries. And with that, some commensurate compensation.

      \n Depends on the ubiquity of copying technology, I suppose. On the other hand, libraries lend DVDs already, but not everyone bothers to copy them, so I would say no, it doesn't affect my opinion. I'm not talking about zero tolerance, but about a practical boundary that protects the creator's investment of blood and sweat.

      The issue boils down to a huge difference between the time and effort required to make a work and the time and effort to copy and distribute the digital version.

      There's a bit of a lemmings to the sea effect going on here where people copy because they can, and the result is that financial structures that support free expression are being destroyed, while the financial structures that support oppression remain untouched. Copyright arguably allowed and encouraged distribution of rock and roll during a period where the prevailing power structure deemed it immoral, because copyright supported an economic model that in turn motivated the spread of this new style of music. Writing a good novel or putting together a solid album or painting a brilliant picture takes months or years of real effort and intellectual and artistic capital. If we don't have economic structures in place to support artists and protect their investment, I think society will lose out. As I said above, I expect we are heading to a new model regardless, and we'll have to live with it. But the mid-list authors will be gone, or lost in an ocean of fanfic quality crap.

      Edited to add: I am not in agreement with this, which is another side effect of pushing too hard against the tide. It's because there's MONEY in copyright infringement.

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Yes, Blake, definitely! I want to clarify - I'm not arguing against you, I'm examining different facets of the issue. Your projections about the future seem rather reasonable; something like that is probably what's in store.

      The important ray of hope for the culture industry in all this is that the fraction of the money going directly to the substantial creator himself will increase due to many different factors involved: not only will the material cost of publication be all but zero once everybody moves to digital media in whatever field of culture one operates, but the reduced competition brought about by free distribution (and less committed artists escaping the market, essentially) will mean that a smaller fraction of the income will go to marketing and other logistic expenses. I'd like to visionarize that the professional creator of the future will subsist on a larger piece of a smaller pie in all regards, while the consumer will perhaps come to appreciate the artist more without the marketing machine that shapes our current relationship to culture. Just imagine how it will influence the way you select the music you listen once there is no corporate interest constantly communicating to you about what is new and what is hot; that's probably where we're going in the digital culture world, as nobody has the money for nearly the current level of - one would like to say artificial - competition.

      What will ultimately kill copy protection is the fact that once a protection has been cracked, it's relatively trivial to transform the digital data into a non-protected package. At that point the consumer can select between buying a protected version or downloading a non-protected one, which means that the only people ever interacting with the copy protection measures are those who buy their products. It's a remarkably desperate proposition to try to protect high-profile stuff with copy protection measures, as it's not the occasional Sunday pirate that your protection has to keep out, but rather the very best the world has to offer - and once that guy cracks the work he's quick to distribute the result. It's a rather daunting task to invent a copy-protection measure that'll hold even months before being routinely crackable.

      There is some light at the end of the tunnel for the pro-copyright crowd as regards technical means of preventing piracy, though: one of the most credible proposals I've heard is to simply lock the everyday computer user into a certain set of verified, secure file formats that can be tracked and certified. The file format alone is not sufficient, as anybody can then just skip using data in that format as is the case with various DRM measures now; for this reason it's imperative to get the hardware and software producers to cooperate in creating computers that simply refuse to run any media software that is not certified as hamstrung (unable to run open media file formats, that is). On a theoretical level this sort of thing can reach a very high level of security, as the user of the computer would be prevented on a hardware level from running any software from unknown sources, thereby effectively preventing them from using third-party media solutions. I seem to remember that this is not just theory anymore, either... ah, Trusted Computing. Great stuff, thrilling to imagine as a widely adopted solution.

      The above sort of solutions are what I mean when I say that the cure is worse than the disease. How do you convince somebody to trade their computer for a model that only runs software certified by the manufacturer? I suppose it's possible, but the way to that is essentially to ban the production of non-controlled computers and prosecute any attempts at directly modifying the hardware to bypass the certification architecture. Furthermore, content creators who actually need to be able to create open and modifiable files as part of their workflow, what about them - they'll need to use software that forcibly watermarks anything they make for tracking purposes, I suppose. And of course anybody wanting to create computer hardware or software would have to get licensed to do so. This is pretty hardcore shit by my measure, considering the various personal freedoms that need to be at least endangered to make it work, and the need to transform the entire computer industry into an intimately state-controlled endeavour.

      (In case it's not clear, the reason for why this sort of piracy-prevention would need to be ubiquitous is that if it's voluntary, then only the law-abiding amateurs will buy a "trusted" computer platform. The nerds who actually know what they're doing with a computer will continue using neutral computing solutions. Might still curb some piracy, at least as long as it takes for the mainstream audience to realize that the computers sold to them are intentionally impaired and worse in actual use than the open platforms.)
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Posted By: HalfjackDoes the fact that libraries are now loaning eBooks affect your view on them?
      \nSome random internet checking suggests that libraries have only a certain number of copies of eBooks and you have to check them out like normal books. If the copies are checked out already, you can put a hold on the book like normal, and wait till it's released.

      The main differences between eBook lending and normal book lending, as far as I can tell, are that eBooks just revert back to the library at the end of the lending period (you don't "return" them) and you're expected to delete the digital copy you have like a good little borrower (there's no practical way for them to delete it for you). In my mind, this is only a little different than letting you photocopy the book (which they really don't do, either, but it happens).

      \n\nAccountingForTaste \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So, here's an "...And You" question for the crowd: do you feel like having a digital copy of something is better, or worse, or exactly the same as having the physical object that was copied?

      In other words: is a directory of mp3s the same to you as a CD? Is a scanned PDF the same as a book? Is an AVI ripped from a movie the same as a DVD?

      If you could have only one or the other, which would you pick -- the digital version or the physical artifact? If you had the one you didn't prefer (say, a scanned PDF) and then saw an opportunity to buy the one you really wanted (the physical book!), what would convince you to buy it?


      For me, the answers vary depending on what we're talking about, and I touched on it on the first page of the thread. PDFs are worse than books in very nearly every possible set of circumstances, AVIs are worse than DVDs (assuming it's a movie I want to keep), but I've grown to prefer digital music to CDs. Given the chance to buy mp3s directly from the artist easily and cheaply, I will happily do so; but given the opportunity to buy a shiny plastic disc from a music publisher, I will likely turn up my nose at it.
      \n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n

      \n
      \n I'll go for the digital file in music, movies, comics and video games. Books are pretty much the only medium that's not quite there yet for me, but that's just because I haven't bought a good e-reader yet. Oh, and newspapers - those I read on paper still, mostly because they come to me on paper.

      My disdain for the physical format is strong in all cases where I've switched to full digital; I don't like caring for material property in general, and if I need to choose between maintaining my gardening toolshed or my CD collection, it's the latter that I'm going to throw away to not have to worry about it. When I need to use physical media in work-related contexts, such as this weird karaoke machine maintenance sideline I've been thrust into lately, my strategy whenever possible is to copy the material over onto general-purpose digital media instead of juggling the original disks.

      \n Digital, I suppose. I enjoy the experience of the physical artifact more, but digital lets me easily index a huge amount of material. Like Eero, I'd prefer to minimize the hassle of caring for and shelving oodles of books, CDs, and DVDs. With books, it could potentially offer the benefit of bespoke formatting.

      Eero, there might also be a cloud solution--not comfortable saying that's any good either, since it doesn't sit right with me. This would be a structure that only lets you access the material on a server via a secure internet connection in (potentially) a locked format you can't copy onto your client. May not be possible with books, though the format may make copying much more challenging, but it would likely work with movies. There's been some talk about doing this with applications, so that everything resides only server-side.

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n As a follow-up question, then...is it important to you to possess the work in a digital format, or are the files themselves essentially disposable? What kinds of things do you keep, and what do you throw away?

      For example, in the physical world, you buy books and newspapers. You keep books indefinitely (or maybe you don't, maybe you sell most of them or give them away to friends when you're done, and only keep a few), but you probably always toss the newspapers in the recycling bin the next day. How would you treat a movie you downloaded? Would you archive it and keep it in case you ever wanted to see it again at some point, or would you just delete it after you'd watched it once?

      More importantly for the people wrangling with the whole selling-creative-works-in-the-world-of-file-sharing problem, what role does how much you paid for the file play in that decision to keep something or throw it away? Or to flip the question a bit, what price would be too high for a file that you didn't get to keep indefinitely?
      \n

      \n\nRon Hammack \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      Posted By: Accounting for TasteSo, here's an "...And You" question for the crowd: do you feel like having a digital copy of something is better, or worse, or exactly the same as having the physical object that was copied?

      In other words: is a directory of mp3s the same to you as a CD? Is a scanned PDF the same as a book? Is an AVI ripped from a movie the same as a DVD?

      If you could have only one or the other, which would you pick -- the digital version or the physical artifact? If you had the one you didn't prefer (say, a scanned PDF) and then saw an opportunity to buy the one you really wanted (the physical book!), what would convince you to buy it?
      \nA directory of mp3s is way better than a CD -- I never have to worry about forgetting to put an mp3 back in its case and getting it all scratched up, or loaning it to a friend and never getting it back, or being tempted to part with it at the used record store when I'm short on cash. AVIs are the same way.

      PDFs, on the other hand... I hate PDFs. Some day in the distant future when they make a e-book reader I can safely toss in my back pocket and take to the beach, read in the bathtub, spill beer on, accidentally leave out in the rain, use as improvised coaster, or kill a bug with I'll change my opinion, but for now I see them as as hideous undead mockeries of real books.

      Now, I still buy plenty of PDFs. You can't beat the convenience of hearing about a cool new game at 3am and being able to get your very own copy ten minutes later. But my joy in reading something new is always tempered by the feeling that I'm constantly beating my head against the medium. And I wouldn't even think of trying to do something as page-flip-intensive as playing a game with a PDF.

      I also illegally download PDFs -- you can't beat the convenience of hearing about a cool new game at 3am and being able to thumb through it at the bookstore to see if you're interested in buying it -- only it's a magic bookstore that exists in your house, has everything in stock, is open at 3 am, and doesn't care if you're wearing pants. Usually I end up deleting the file half an hour later after looking through it and deciding it's not for me, just like most of the times I thumb through an RPG book at the bookstore I end up putting it back on the shelf after deciding I won't get enough use out of it to justify the cover price. And I wouldn't even think about doing something as dickish as actually playing a game I didn't legitimately own. Especially considering how ridiculously cheap of a hobby playing roleplaying games* is.

      (*As distinguished from often-related and vastly more expensive hobby of collecting roleplaying games.)

    File Sharing and You

    \n
    \xab123456\xbb

    Comments

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Posted By: HexabolicEero, there might also be a cloud solution--not comfortable saying that's any good either, since it doesn't sit right with me. This would be a structure that only lets you access the material on a server via a secure internet connection in (potentially) a locked format you can't copy onto your client. May not be possible with books, though the format may make copying much more challenging, but it would likely work with movies. There's been some talk about doing this with applications, so that everything resides only server-side.
      \nThe problem here is that unless the receiving computer is also controlled by the distributor, it's relatively simple to route the data into a permanent file - in fact, it's theoretically impossible to have a file format that can be played but can't be copied without limiting the file only to certified playback devices, as the copying can always happen at the output end even if nowhere else. The very crudest way of making such a copy of a DRM'd movie would be to set up a camera in front of your tv and tape whatever is shown; a ridiculously primitive solution, of course, but illustrative of the theoretical problem of creating a truly closed file format that is still able to be used. Not trivial to crack a good closed format of course, and I couldn't do it myself at the drop of a hat, but it's sufficient for one person to be able to do if they also decide to make the file available to others. That's the thing about piracy in action, consistent DRM across all copies of the work only works to prevent people from making personal copies; it does nothing to prevent them from downloading the same thing off the Internet from somebody who cracked it for them. In this regard all the nuances of DRM technology are irrelevant to the pirate end-user, he's just enjoying the cracked fruits of somebody else's ingenuity anyway.

      Computer programs, incidentally, are a thing that can apparently be effectively or near-effectively secured, especially in a could-computing set-up. This is because the program itself is not its output, which the case is with static entertainment; you can't "record" a computer program simply by recording what it outputs on your screen. Thus getting a copy of a program that runs on a server somewhere instead of just getting hold of its interface and output would require getting onto that server somehow, which is a different order of computer problem altogether, and one without any ready-made answers. Perhaps video games are going to get a massive lift as the last commercially viable form of culture industry once the fight against piracy is abandoned; everybody will be playing them when Disney puts their marketing machine on it
      \n

      Posted By: DMGnomeWhat I want to know is, how do you react to file sharing? Do you do it? If so, do you have any limitations you set for yourself? Those of you who create, whether you write games or books or music or what-have-you: how does file sharing affect what you do? Do you expect people to pirate your creations? Do you lower your prices? Raise them? Give away your stuff for free?

      Of course, I'm not perfect. I have, on occasion, downloaded things which I could have gotten legally without too much trouble. I find it much easier to demonize corporate entities than independent publishers, so I'm much more likely to pirate from an enormous corporation than from a small independent game designer. What about you?
      \nI'm not a creator, so that part of the question doesn't apply to me. If I were, and I was creating work I expected to be compensated for, I'd want anyone who stole it prosecuted to the fullest extent of the law, just like when someone thinks it's funny to walk out of my store with a book slipped in their trenchcoat.

      For my part, I have never used file sharing, and don't expect I ever will. In my mind, it's the same thing as walking into a shop and walking out with merchandise you didn't pay for.

      I find the rationalization that it's better to take food off the table of someone working full-time at Microsoft or WotC somehow "more ethical" than taking food off the table of a small press creator who doesn't actually make a full-time living off that work bizarre. Stealing is stealing. I know many people who work at those so-called 'corporate entities', and using the market cap of their employers as a self-serving justification for my poor impulse control is grossly disingenuous.

      I don't see any difference for out-of-print or hard-to-find work. My stealing it is still entirely a self-serving act that I know to be illegal. I want it yeah, but there's a lot of things in life I want that I know to be wrong, or illegal, and as a result, I don't get them. Shit, I don't even own any PORN I haven't paid the copyright holders for.

      But I also know that all psychologists will tell you that the likelihood of a person to commit a crime is not the severity of the punishment, or even who it affects, but the likelihood of getting caught. As long as it's so EASY to steal other people's intellectual property and get away with it, people will do it because they want it and it's easy, and they're selfish, and make up whatever justification makes sense that week (or is fashionable amongst the 'pirates') afterward.

      -Jim Crocker de la Mancha

      \n\nJoshua A.C. Newman \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Matthew,

      \n
      \n

      I have uninformed opinions on this subject that no one wants or needs to hear,

      \n
      \n

      That's not stopping anyone else. This conversation, with the exceptions of a couple of comments by Ara, Adam, Eero, and Jason, completely a matter of shouting uninformed opinion. So don't let that stop you!

      \n
      \n

      Posted By: cwilbur
      \n I mean, to take an example: it is possible for someone, right this minute, to send $18 to Vincent Baker, and within a day, have a shiny PDF of Apocalypse World in my hands -- and the marginal cost of that PDF to him is measured in pennies.

      \n
      \n

      I blame the public school system. No, actually, I blame the complete and total dissociation of creator from creation that industrial media has brought us.

      \n

      OK, people, this is how it works:

      \n

      You have an idea. You refine the idea. Then you produce an expression of that idea, using everything you learned from the failures (major and minor) from your previous expressions. Then you determine how to best get people to notice and care about your expression. ALL OF THIS IS UNPAID INVESTMENT because what happens next is that someone gives you twenty measly dollars for it. All that work you put in on spec comes back twenty dollars at a time. In the case of Apocalypse World, it's the last 18 months or so of testing and design, plus the ideas and expressions that preceded it that didn't work, plus stuff Vincent's learned from In a Wicked Age, Dogs in the Vineyard, The Dragon Killer, Mechaton, Otherkind, The Abductinators, Nighttime Animals Save the World, and probably another couple I haven't thought of.

      \n

      That "0 margin" PDF is how Vincent ever gets a return

      \n

      Here, everybody. I have an exercise for you:

      \n

      Go make something and sell it. In that time, don't buy a beer (or whatever it is, just something you like and have around) from your cash or regular bank account. Only buy beer from the PayPal account that you use to sell your stuff. I don't know how much beer you'll be able to buy in a few years \u2014\xa0I hope it's a lot! \u2014 but it won't be any for months or years. And I guarantee you that if your product is only in PDF, you'll be drinking a lot less beer, so if you want to drink beer then, you should probably start by putting $500 in that PayPal account so you'll be able to do a print run. Now imagine that that's how you pay your bills.

      \n

      Seriously, do that exercise. It's not a hypothetical.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nJoshua A.C. Newman \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      For me, the notion that I could bust my ass for a year or two, sell my book on a royalty agreement, then have it copied and distributed without compensation is offensive. While it may not technically rise to the legal definition of theft, I nevertheless feel victimized. It feels like the moral equivalent of theft. Sure, it's not the same level of intrusion as violation of your home, but the creative work is your baby, and if it's copied without permission, it's essentially a theft of my time and effort. If I choose to release my work for free, that's one thing. If I don't, then an unauthorized and uncompensated copy constitutes, in my view, a parasitic taking. It ignores the labor that went into the book. It ignores the fact that the limit on copyright is part of the creator's choice to control the supply of the product that hits the market. It potentially impacts my income and my ability to feed my family using my hard-won professional skill. I can still sell my writing as a service, just not as a good. I suspect this is the future for professional creative writing.

      \n
      \n

      Sadly, Blake, you're exactly the last person to benefit from your writing. The question is "How do we get the money into your hands as the creator?" not "How do we keep people from taking the book without paying the retailer, distributor, publisher, publisher's employees, and then me?"

      \n

      The primary arguments against IP law are really against the middlemen, not you. But middlemen love to hold you up as a human shield to protect their middlemannery.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\n14thWarrior \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Posted By: Joshua A.C. Newman"How do we keep people from taking the book without paying the retailer, distributor, publisher, publisher's employees, and then me?"
      \nI think one place to start is to foster a culture in which people willingly reward the producers of consumed media for the benefit they receive. I'm presuming that the media producer in this scenario isn't railing against the fact that their media is freely available on the internet.

      Many webcomics creators and other web media creators already put Paypal Donation buttons on their websites for consumers to donate whatever they feel is a fair value. This model can work, and can likely work well if a culture of voluntary reward payment is fostered.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\ncwilbur \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n There's a reason I used the word "marginal cost" rather than "cost" in my example. The sunk cost of Apocalypse World probably comes to hundreds, if not thousands, of hours. The marginal cost of one more PDF copy, however, is pennies.

      The point of the concrete example, though, was *not* "the marginal cost of AW PDFs is pennies, so he should sell them for a lot less." No, it's that $18 for a PDF gives him considerably more profit per copy sold than he gets under the traditional author-publisher-distributor-retailer system. Suppose we fix the sunk cost of AW at $50,000. How many PDFs does he need to sell, in the neighborhood of $15 profit on each sale, to recoup that? Under the model in place twenty years ago, how many books would he have needed to sell in the traditional model, in the neighborhood of $3-4 profit on each sale, to recoup that? (And that's even assuming he could have found a publisher, that the distributors would have ordered the game, that the retail stores would have stocked it....)

      The same developments in technology that make it possible for AW to be so easily obtained on file sharing networks *also* make it possible for AW to be so easily obtained legitimately, and also funnel a great deal of the profits straight to the creator rather than to middlemen. Twenty years ago? Merchant accounts for credit card processing. Today? PayPal accounts. The changes that make the copying possible *also* make your PayPal account, for beer money or bill-paying money, possible.

      This is the reason that (as another concrete example) Steve Jackson Games is moving so much of their GURPS line to PDFs. The cost of developing the content in that PDF is exactly the same -- but there are no manufacturing or warehousing costs and no up-front investment in inventory. And the marginal profit is substantially higher, since instead of involving an author, a publisher, a distributor, a retailer, and a purchaser, the process involves the author, the publisher, and the purchaser. This means that a product with an order of magnitude *smaller* sales figures can be *more* profitable because it is digital and not text.

      It also ignores one of the very concrete examples I gave. Within the past month, I gave an unauthorized copy of In A Wicked Age to a friend so she could read it, *and she bought a copy.* I also gave an unauthorized copy of Remember Tomorrow to a friend so he could read it after having played a convention game, *and he bought a copy*. This also was not possible 20 years ago.

      And, honestly, we're still in the middle of the changes. Portable readers are getting better and cheaper. I already buy things both in print and PDF versions because there are situations in which each is more convenient; but I can see a day coming when the main reason I buy a print book is nostalgia.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Many webcomics creators and other web media creators already put Paypal Donation buttons on their websites for consumers to donate whatever they feel is a fair value. This model can work, and can likely work well if a culture of voluntary reward payment is fostered.

      \n
      \n

      They also make most of their money on tangible goods \u2014\xa0T shirts, books, and the like.

      \n

      Every so often, Cat and Girl runs a "Donation Derby", where she gives away weird shit to people who donate. It's a very personal, and very Girl, way of doing it.

      \n Here's a question that I'm wondering about. I'm posing it to Jim Crocker specifically, because he's made the analogy between unauthorized copying and shoplifting, and I'm curious about his answer, but anyone else can comment. (Like I could stop you.)

      Suppose there was a technological development that meant every book you sold brought you somewhere between two and three times as much profit per sale, without increasing the cost to the purchaser, but it meant that if someone walked out of your store with a book under his or her overcoat, there was nothing you could do about it and you would just lose that book. Would that development be worth using?

      What I'm most interested in here is the reasoning behind the answer.

      \n\nWordman \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010

      \n I admit to being totally baffled what people think the goal of this conversation is, so at the risk of making it even more obscure, I'll just mention the following: some of the ethical logic being employed here is based on the notion that pirating something hurts the creator because it does not compensate them. And, certainly, this is often true. On the other hand, it also (and, I'd guess, much more often) isn't. The problem is that the "piracy" == "creator harm" equation only holds if the pirate would otherwise have paid for the work. The likelihood that the average pirate who torrents, say, the entire library of Shadowrun products would have otherwise paid for every single item in that library seems vanishingly small. Conversely, it's pretty likely that he would have paid for some of them. So, it may be that the entire download is unethical, but only some of that download is actually causing real "harm". (Naturally, no legal system is equipped to deal with nebulous things like what someone might have done in some alternate universe, so this obviously isn't a legal point, or approach.)

      To make the whole thing even more ridiculous, consider what happens if the situation is exactly the same, except what is being pirated isn't a creative work, but rather a tangible, consumable object, such as an apple. That is, suppose the pirate has a magical machine where an apple can be uploaded, and then anyone can download and eat a copy of the apple. You still have the same situation. The creator of the original apple is not being compensated for the copy, but any given copy might not actually chance the creator's finances, since the pirate might not have consumed the apple at all if piracy were not available. And yet, in this case, the pirate probably paid something for the copy of the apple. You might imagine, for example, that it took some amount of energy to create the copy. Chances are that the pirate paid for that energy. You can assume that, absent the piracy, this energy may not have been used. So, you have an energy company gaining profit that it would not have otherwise gained, because of the piracy. In effect, the energy company is profiting from the creator's work, without compensating her. The same could probably be said about the network provider who supplies the pirate with his connectivity, likely for a price. Are they harming the creator? Does that harm, if it exists, depend on if the pirate would have otherwise paid for the orange? Is it a zero-sum game? That is, if there is harm, is it costing the creator as much as the power company and the pirate are gaining? If these costs are not equal, is society as a whole gaining or loosing?

      \u03ba\u03b1\u03bb\u03bb\u03b9\u03c7\u03c4\u03b9

      \n There certainly IS something you can do about shoplifting. Prevent it. I run a game retail store in my primary position now and I can assure you, preventing shoplifting is a key part of the job.
      \n
      \nThe store is laid out so as to avoid exposure to small pocketable items in out of sight places, the cash wrap is laid out as to be central on the floorplan and thusly can see most everywhere in the store, small items are inventoried higher up where they are more easily seen my staff, used and low value product is nearest the door, blistered miniatured packs are behind lock cases with plexiglass fronts for product viewing, etc, etc.
      \n
      \nAnd when someone is found to be trying to shoplift, they are detained and the police are called. And then we make sure the local prosecutor actually presecutes or at least pleads them out.
      \n
      \nAs a consequence, our shoplifting loss is less than 1/4 of 1%, which is very good by retail averages.
      \n
      \nAny retail store that doesn't pay any attention to shoplifting is a retail store that will ultimately go out of business. Even retail stores that do pay attention, but ultimately let their shrinkage grow to large tend to get closed by their corporate parents. Not usually without first replacing the store manager a few times first though.
      \n
      \nThere are entire swaths of Metro detroit where you can not find a single on the street retail business. Shoplifting and robberies caused them all the close. Now people living in those parts of the city must drives miles even to find a simple thing like a convenience store. Intellectual property creators can easily be "shoplifted" out of business as well, then the people will simply have to learn to live without, or create everything for themselves.
      \n
      \nThanks,
      \nRyan S. Johnson
      \nGuild of Blades Retail Group - http://www.gobretail.com
      \nGuild of Blades Publishing Group - http://www.guildofblades.com
      \n1483 Online - http://www.1483online.com

      \n\nPaulCzege \n\n\n

      \n Online conversations about piracy of created works always focus on whether creators are being financially harmed. Almost certainly this is because that's the way civil courts look at things, and piracy falls under the umbrella of the civil courts. For me though, the problem is that the piracy of created works is culturally destructive. If I ask you to do something for me, we're creating a relationship. The presence of people in relationships is what makes a society. Me asking you to pay me a fair price for something is us participating in society. The piracy of created works severs the connection between the creator and the work. And then neither the pirate nor the creator are fully participating in the human endeavor.

      Paul
      \n
      \n

      \n\nWordman \n\n\n

      \n
      \n
      Posted By: Paul CzegeFor me though, the problem is that the piracy of created works is culturally destructive.
      \nI don't buy that.

      Counterexample: Forty-one years ago, there was this concert on a farm. Many people pirated tickets to this concert (either by actually forging copies or just by sneaking in). Eventually the people running the concert just surrendered and let anyone in. In spite of this (or, I submit, because of this) Woodstock became a defining cultural moment.

      The problem I have with the premise is that the "the piracy of created works severs the connection between the creator and the work" isn't the whole story. If it was, there would be no point in seeing a painting in an art museum (or, hell, on the street). Once a viewer/reader sees a work, this creates a new connection, regardless of the presence (or desires) of the creator. A pirated copy, simply by virtue of being seen by people, creates culture. That this culture might not be the culture the creator wanted doesn't necessarily mean it is "destructive".

      In the situation you describe (where "me asking you to pay me a fair price for something is us participating in society"), you could view the "fair price" as something which creates a barrier to culture. In essence, the price says that "only some may benefit from my work". Others, namely those unwilling to pay the price, cannot benefit from it. Viewed through that lens, it is commerce, not piracy, that is "culturally destructive", preventing some that could have gained something from the work from "fully participating in the human endeavor".
      \n

      Posted By: Paul CzegeFor me though, the problem is that the piracy of created works is culturally destructive. If I ask you to do something for me, we're creating a relationship. The presence of people in relationships is what makes a society. Me asking you to pay me a fair price for something is us participating in society.       \nI didn't know where to clip that, because it's all a piece.

      I disagree if you say culturally destructive, but if you say socially destructive I have a much harder time arguing with it.

      And I think your stance works really well when we're talking about creators and consumers who are people -- to go back to my old example, there's something corrosive in my choice to download Apocalypse World rather than to buy it. (This is not a choice I made - it's a hypothetical choice. When I was in that position, I bought it.) But that's because it's a game made with hundreds to thousands of hours of work, and the $18 for the PDF is less than I earn in an hour of work, and what I am saying by refusing to pay that is that I value Apocalypse World less than I value two crappy summer movies, less than two unhealthy fast food meals -- and since I value it more than that, I have to lie to myself to justify the copy. If I copy AW instead of paying for it, Vincent is out $18 minus PayPal fees, and that's not fair or reasonable.

      But here, we're talking about creators. If I buy a $25 copy of GURPS Space, how much do the creators see of that? I'd bet less than $3, with the other $20 going to middlemen. If the idea is to see that the creators get compensation for their work, they're better off if I download an illegitimate copy and send them a check for $10. Further, when I give $18 to the creator of Apocalypse World for his work, it creates a relationship. When I give $25 to a website for a copy of GURPS Space, and quarterly that website gives $3 per copy sold to the authors, there's really no relationship there.

      \n\nAccountingForTaste \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010
      \n

      \n
      Posted By: cwilburFurther, when I give $18 to the creator of Apocalypse World for his work, it creates a relationship. When I give $25 to a website for a copy of GURPS Space, and quarterly that website gives $3 per copy sold to the authors, there's really no relationship there.
      \nThat idea has been mentioned by musicians coping with the rise in file sharing -- rather than selling people a shiny plastic disc, you sell them on the connection between them, their music, you, and everyone else who buys in. You cultivate a fanbase who will support you and promote you to their friends and acquaintances. But that obviously only works if the artist wants to put energy into building and maintaining that relationship with his/her fans (and asking for their money!), and if the fans are satisfied with that kind of relationship (and consider it worth spending cash on). It can't work for everyone, and there are musicians and fans who were perhaps happier with, and better served by, the old model: the reclusive and mysterious artist creates the music, the record label distributes it and handles the icky business of getting money out of people, and the fans get to feel like the artist is pure and unspoiled and making the music out of pure joy unmarred by crass material concerns. Or, less pejoratively, the artists get to put more energy into creating art, because they're not busy putting energy into creating a profitable relationship with fans and doing all the insanely labor-intensive self-promotion.


      But I do think that, especially these days, people like to know where the money they're spending is actually going, no matter what business model is being used. If you're told something will cost you $10, you think "Do I have $10, and would I rather spend this money on something else?" But if you're told something costs $10, of which $1 goes to cover the transaction fees, $3 goes to the vendor who's selling it, and $6 goes to the creator...that adds an awareness of the people on the other side of the purchase. It's not just me and my ten bucks anymore, it's me giving my ten bucks to people. And that's kind of a relationship, even if I never get a handwritten thank-you note from the creator or any other kind of acknowledgment. It appeals to the moralist in me, if nothing else.

      (That this kind of pricing transparency would also expose the gross inequities of some business models is an attractive side effect, in my opinion. I think a lot of consumers would be appalled at how little some creators get paid in comparison to the middlemen who arguably add little or nothing of value to the product.)
      \n
