      \n Adam Dray: sure, the middlemen and distributors are people too. It's just that very few of them actually add any value to the product, and yet take the majority of the purchase price. Just being people doesn't mean that they deserve a larger share of my money than the creator who creates what I wanted to buy in the first place.

      Paul tried to argue that the main reason to not make unauthorized copies is because it harms relationships. For that to work, there has to be a relationship subject to harm in the first place -- and the work-for-hire employee of the publisher who sold something to the distributor who sold something to a retailer relationship is more distant a relationship than the fictional friend-of-a-friend in urban legends. If there's no actual relationship, it can't be harmed.

      And yes, I'd *totally* be in favor of complete pricing transparency. "This book costs $45, of which $22 goes to the store you're standing in, $11 goes to the distributor and warehouser, $4 goes to the printer, $7 goes to the publisher, and $1 goes to the author who actually wrote the words you just shelled out $45 for."

      Posted By: HexabolicDoctorow may be making enough to live off his writing (I don't know if he actually is), but I don't know any other writers seeing enough sales from eBooks to support them as writers. The folks I'm talking to--widely published and making a full-time living today off dead tree publication--aren't reporting electronic sales as anything more than a blip, not even a promising trend. I'm highly skeptical that living on the long tail will enable more than a tiny percentage of writers to earn nontrivial income.
      It sounds like you're misunderstanding what Cory Doctorow does. He doesn't make ANY monkey on e-books, because he gives away electronic and even audiobook versions of his work for free. I, too, have no idea whether he lives on his book sales or not, but one thing he seems to be saying is that not only can't he stop people from sharing/copying/pirating electronic versions of his work but he doesn't want to because doing so would be: impossible, pointless (because he sees them more as marketing to help spread knowledge of him and his work), and even unethical (because he considers copying to be a natural urge).
      You didn't name names about the folks you're talking to, so I can't say whether I've heard of them or not. If I haven't, I might suggest that, at least if Cory Doctorow is correct, if they released electronic versions of their work for free, more people, like me, might discover them and become new fans and customers. Maybe. I have absolutely no evidence at all that what Cory Doctorow does works. But I definitely think he's awesome because of it.

      Posted By: cwilburHere's a question that I'm wondering about. I'm posing it to Jim Crocker specifically, because he's made the analogy between unauthorized copying and shoplifting, and I'm curious about his answer, but anyone else can comment. (Like I could stop you.)

      Suppose there was a technological development that meant every book you sold brought you somewhere between two and three times as much profit per sale, without increasing the cost to the purchaser, but it meant that if someone walked out of your store with a book under his or her overcoat, there was nothing you could do about it and you would just lose that book. Would that development be worth using?

      What I'm most interested in here is the reasoning behind the answer.
      \nNo. Because the moment people figured out that there were no enforceable consequences for shoplifting, it'd soar. The level of shrinkage we'd see would quickly offset the increased profit.

      Keep in mind, also, that just as there are fine distinctions I missed between 'sunk' cost and 'marginal' cost, there's also a huge difference between gross and net profit. Gross profit on a typical hobby game is in the 45-50% range. NET profit, after rent, fixed costs like utilities, payroll, and yes, shrinkage, run in the 5-10% range.

      -Jim C.

      Posted By: cwilburAdam Dray: sure, the middlemen and distributors are people too. It's just that very few of them actually add any value to the product, and yet take the majority of the purchase price. Just being people doesn't mean that they deserve a larger share of my money than the creator who creates what I wanted to buy in the first place.
      \nObviously, as a sleazy and useless Middleman, I am as biased in the other direction as the creators are in this one.

      That said, it's always going to be a question of scale: if the distribution network provides you economies of scale and throw weight that outstrip your ability to do it on your own, then they add value. As folks like Josh have and Rob have attested as well, it's possible to be selective about WHICH middlemen add value of a sort you find useful. It may well be that selling your entire print run to Alliance no longer makes sense. It may be that having your games on display in the couple of dozen progressive and activist game stores around the country is worth taking a margin hit in exchange for the exposure. The days of throwing stuff into the channel and hoping it takes off are going away, and even I don't think that's necessarily bad.

      The other problem we always run into in these conversations is the presumption that all consumers are as pro-active and versed in the arcana of the industry as everyone here is. What a GOOD 'middleman' can do is put that product into the hands and in front of the eyes of people who would otherwise never see it. That service is going to be worth different amounts to different people, sure, but it shouldn't be dismissed as worthless on the face any more than its value should be presumed.

      -Jim C.
      \n

      \n\nJim_Crocker \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Posted By: cwilburIf I buy a $25 copy of GURPS Space, how much do thecreatorssee of that?
      \nNone. Something like GURPS Space is written as a piece of contract work. The creator is paid a lump sum upon delivery of the work, and MAYBE a tiny royalty upon future printings. The success or failure of the piece determines whether that author receives future contract work. A decision not to purchase it doesn't affect directly whether he eats or not on THAT project, but it has a big effect on future contracts he might (or might not) receive.

      I say this because I actually know the guy who wrote GURPS Space, or at least one version of it.

      -Jim C.

      Posted By: Jim CrockerThe other problem we always run into in these conversations is the presumption that all consumers are as pro-active and versed in the arcana of the industry as everyone here is. What a GOOD 'middleman' can do is put that product into the hands and in front of the eyes of people who would otherwise never see it. That service is going to be worth different amounts to different people, sure, but it shouldn't be dismissed as worthless on the face any more than its value should be presumed.
      \nI actually have good feelings about some "middlemen" companies...mostly when the people who use them specifically call them out as providing particularly good service at a reasonable price. I sort of wish that this was the rule rather than the exception, and that you didn't have to be paying close attention to know that (for example) a musician gets a bigger share of the price of a download from Bandcamp.com than they do from iTunes or Amazon, or how much work some company really is doing to get a product out there and how genuinely supportive they are, or whatever. I suppose I just wish that finding out that information wasn't so uncommon, or so hard to do.

      There's probably a line where useful transparency becomes Too Much Information, but most of the time I feel like we're miles and miles away from that line.
      \n

      \n\ncwilbur \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Posted By: Jim CrockerObviously, as a sleazy and useless Middleman, I am as biased in the other direction as the creators are in this one.
      \nThere are good middlemen and bad middlemen: good middlemen more than earn their portion of the fee by functioning as curators and promoters. It seems to me that a store owner who talks to a customer, gets to know the customer's tastes, and says, "I have the *perfect* book for you," well, that owner is more than earning his share. Or if he actively promotes stuff: the difference between wholesale and retail on game books also pays for gaming space and demos.

      But numerically speaking, especially once you get out of the specialty shops, those good middlemen are few and far between. Chris Aylott, at the Space-Crime Continuum, could tell me when I walked into the shop what games and books I should look at, and that service would have been cheap at twice the price. (In something like nine years, he was only ever wrong in a recommendation once.) I imagine something similar happens in your shop, too. But when I walk into a Borders or Barnes and Noble, that level of service is simply unavailable, and there are a *lot* more Borders stores than there are awesome local bookstores.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Posted By: fnord3125Are we talking past each other, Adam? My hypothetical situation is this: I take your work and I incorporate it into some kind of work of artistic expression that you, personally, don't like. If you recognize it as art, and not just as an attack on you, but you dislike it, would you protest and/or sue to stop it?
      \nI don't think specified that it was a) art and b) not an attack on me. I have no idea where you're going with this, though, and I feel like you're asking leading questions. But I'll play, trusting you in good faith.

      I can always protest to the artist, even if it's art and even if it's not an attack on me. I don't think there's anything with protesting to the artist. That's just two people talking.

      I would not sue to stop someone if I felt they were using my work within Fair Use, which probably includes the situation you've described. I'm really not a litigious person.\n
      Posted By: cwilburAdam Dray: sure, the middlemen and distributors are people too. It's just that very few of them actually add any value to the product, and yet take the majority of the purchase price. Just being people doesn't mean that they deserve a larger share of my money than the creator who creates what I wanted to buy in the first place.
      \nThey add value to the author. Maybe not always, but often. There's a reason not everyone goes indie. Even if you just point at the near distribution monopoly they have (for brick and mortar stores, anyway), they worked hard to build those business relationships. But they do more than that.

      My wife is a professional fiction author, just now breaking into the scene. She's being published by two "real" New York publishing houses. It'll be five trade paperbacks and eight novella eBooks over the next couple years, plus whatever new contracts she manages to land. /brag

      She has to do much of the marketing herself. It's really a pain in the ass, we're finding. She's had to learn about layout and printers and binding and stuff to make excerpt books. She is getting to the point where she just wants to pay people to do this stuff for her, and she's generally a real penny pincher.

      Her publishers are providing her with real value. They've taken a financial risk on her work, paying her an advance on royalties (as pretty much every author gets). They've edited her work. They've provided cover art (and for her Berkley book, this involved a model search, photographers, painters, etc.). They will print the books and ship them to book stores. They will promote the work and handle a certain amount of the publicity, though she is expected to do a good deal of this herself (as a new author, anyway).

      I don't understand why people seem to hate the "middlemen" so much. Yes, they take a large chunk of change. They also do a lot and take risks. They can provide an economy of scale that individuals cannot achieve.

      I'm not poopooing on the indie publishing scene either. I love and support indie publishing. I love seeing real competition to the old multitier model. But I'd be sad if the big guys went away entirely, or the indie people went away entirely. They both serve a purpose.

      And I doubt most people care where the dollars go. Maybe a certain class of internet person. I think most people just want to know how much they have to pay to have the thing they want.\n
      Posted By: cwilburBut when I walk into a Borders or Barnes and Noble, that level of service is simply unavailable, and there are a *lot* more Borders stores than there are awesome local bookstores.
      \nThey provide another service though: warehousing a stockpile of books that you can go see and touch before you buy them. Also, letting you have a book /right now/, not 2-3 days shipping from now. If this isn't valuable to you, then you obviously have no need for bookstores. I stop in my local B&N occasionally and it's always hopping, so people obviously still find it valuable. I also support my local "small business" bookstore and my local FLGS. These things provide a value to me beyond book recommendations.

      \n Wondering aloud, and bypassing the bickering:

      \n\nHexabolic \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n

      \n Barnes & Noble and Borders between them handle something like 70% of the books sold in the U.S. Like it or not, that's considerable middleman throw-weight, especially if your book is featured in a more visible location. My friend Patty Briggs, who writes urban fantasy, had her first novel (in that genre) displayed on a weekend when the next Twilight book was due out. As it happened, the Twilight book was delayed, but word didn't reach the fans, who mobbed the stores and ended up picking up Patty's book instead. Now her books are consistently hitting #1 on the NYT bestseller list. I haven't talked to her about ebooks. Obviously lightning struck in her case, but she definitely benefited from the middleman positioning.

      Elizabeth, I think your idea sounds lovely, sort of a shareware model. In practice, I think it's a high-trust model that depends on both creator and consumer being honest, direct, and ethical, which may be unrealistic to expect from everyone in a practical sense. Further, I suspect it would break down if the numbers increased past a certain threshold. The time to reach closure on each transaction becomes both attenuated and potentially much higher, depending on how long it takes for the communication.

      Here's an example: we shipped the original Tribes in 1998 without DRM, and it spread like wildfire, becoming hugely popular. Unfortunately, very few of those fans bought the game, so Sierra received very little money on the title. By very little, I mean "took a significant loss." If Tribes 2 hadn't screwed the proverbial canine, we might have seen profit on that game based on the wide penetration from the first, but that's really a hypothetical. We don't know how long the anti-piracy measures on that game would have held out, or what impact piracy would have had. The production cost of T2 meant that we had to see astronomical sales to break even. So in this case, most fans who loved the game and played it incessantly (based on our survey of the server numbers over time) didn't support the creators.

      I think middlemen often do add quite a bit of value. I try to buy local when I can, and I educate myself about the goods and services I get, but I don't expect a high-fidelity relationship with everyone in my supply web. I mean, I still buy organic from out of state, or even out of country. I don't always buy the local product if it's significantly more expensive than the equivalent from another source. As an author, even when you have the assistance of a publishing house, there's a lot of marketing and promotion work to do. If you're self-published, that promotional slice of your day becomes significantly larger.

      I took my opinion from Doctorow's essay, in which (as I recall) he implied he made more by giving his work away for free than by selling it. I don't have a beef with him, and I appreciate free stuff as much as the next guy, but he's choosing to do that. No one is appropriating his stuff without his permission or against his wishes. That's the moral crux of this discussion, I think.

      In some ways, the argument for digital works being free and unprotected on account of the marginal reproduction cost approaching zero seems to say that the lower the marginal cost of production, the greater the moral justification for simply taking a unit of whatever without compensating the producer.

      Here's a question: if we could implement a moderate technological DRM solution that worked in most cases without hugely authoritarian or intrusive means, say some sort of file key-lock that actually held out against modern decryption without slogging your experience with the content, and thus the genie of cost-free digital reproduction was put back in the bottle, would that change anyone's outlook?

      \n\ncwilbur \n\n\n

      \n In my case? No, a hypothetically perfect DRM solution -- or even a hypothetical DRM solution that's no less intrusive than the physicality of books -- would not change my outlook, because I think copyright as currently constructed is broken. (More generally, I don't think such a DRM solution is possible.)

      To summarize my point of view: copyright is a deal where society says, you have the right to determine who copies this in exchange for your duty to make it available to people who want it for a reasonable price. When you aren't making it available for a reasonable price, you aren't holding up your end of the bargain, so society doesn't have any obligation to hold up its end of the bargain. The ethical thing to do is to pay the creator what he or she asks, provided he or she is asking.

      (If the creator wants to ask $25 so that he can pass on $22 to the marketers and middlemen, that's his business, not mine. I don't think most middlemen add much value, and if I think a thing is worth paying $25 for, I'd rather pay $25 to the creator rather than $2 to the creator and $23 to assorted middlemen, but it's up to the creator what sort of deals he negotiates and what parts of the work he'd rather pay someone else to do for him.)

      Posted By: HexabolicHere's a question:if we could implement a moderate technological DRM solution that worked in most cases without hugely authoritarian or intrusive means, say some sort of file key-lock that actually held out against modern decryption without slogging your experience with the content, and thus the genie of cost-free digital reproduction was put back in the bottle, would that change anyone's outlook?
      \nIt comes as no surprise that it would change mine. Were something like that available, copyright would be rather viable once more. I wouldn't still advocate hunting pirates, but then you wouldn't need to because pirated works would again be occasional rarities, the distribution networks would starve for the lack of product, customers would flock back to commercial outlets with their better product selections and people would grow consistently accustomed to paying for stuff once more. Meanwhile our view of copyright as something real and substantial would be renewed as copyright would, in fact, be just that. With time people would no doubt grow to consider their digital files as real property with collectible value, just like the industry would like them to.

      As we've touched on, though, I find this prospect of successful DRM almost impossible technologically due to how the information networks work. You just need one person able to crack the given DRM, after which it's easy for him to let the work loose for everybody else to distribute. That's an awful lot of cracking power. Less popular properties don't get cracked automatically of course, but it seems that anything with a significant following and a digital representation has so far been successfully pirated, with no exceptions.

      The property-like rights in cultural work come from the ability to sustain the idea both juridically and practically, as I've outlined in this thread. Protect copyright effectively, and it becomes a feasible basis upon which to build the interactions and properties of the culture industry. Break it down as has effectively been done, and we face the daunting prospect of downscaling this culture industry of ours into pre-copyright levels once more, getting rid of the structures that have been built to depend on copyright.

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Ryan, you've approached this thing from the viewpoint of social contract, so I'm wondering: how do you find a creator's right to compensation different from a grass mower's right to the same? The bit that's not working for me in your insistence on societal rights to payment is that when I mow your lawn I'm entitled to a payment only if we have a prior contract on it; if I do it without you asking, you are not obligated to pay me despite you deriving benefit from it. Why is it that a creator making something and relinquishing his effective control over his creation (by publishing it) gets to expect a payment? I can totally understand how this works when copyright is enforceable, and how this works when a creator tragically thinks that copyright is enforceable, but to me it seems utterly screwy that you would impose an active duty onto others to make a business model viable when it no longer is. Morally, on an individual level there might be some basis for being annoyed or even despaired at the lack of empathy on the parts of your fellow men, but that's no news - society doesn't function based on empathy exactly because that tends to benefit those with none.

      Even better simile: if I'm a baker and insist that my business model is to truck all of my produce to the curb every morning for anybody to take with no security whatsoever, I'm sure that the people taking the bread are still morally obligated to pay me, but is it realistic or useful for me to act aggrieved when they don't? Is the baker justified in his anger by the fact that this business model used to work before, when his business was situated in a nice little hippie commune where everybody knew everybody else and the honor system was intact and he could personally sit by his cart of produce and keep track of who is taking his bread? It worked once, so I'm entitled to having it work forevermore? This is a very high-spirited position, but I tend towards more fiscal realism myself simply because a mere moral stand is not sufficient for creating a society between people not controlled by moral principles alone. Thinking it is leads to highly theoretical societal constructs such as communism or any of your favourite utopias; surely great places to live, and they would function perfectly if only everybody always did the right thing.
      \n

      \n\njhkim \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Here are my two cents:

      I feel a good analogy for the issue is a big area of open land owned by a society. One extreme positions would be it should be a public park - everyone collectively helps maintain and develop it, and everyone freely enjoys it. This value the land as free and open territory, and think that fences and patrols detract from the park, even if they are over sections that wouldn't otherwise be used. Another extreme position would be that it should be parceled out as entirely private property - with those who do work on a section of land own it, and the landscape is primarily these private parcels. These views are fundamentally opposed. Those who want the land public are annoyed since they work for the common good, while others benefit from the public land yet fence off the land that they work on.

      I think land is a better analogy for two reasons. Unlike physical goods, there is a huge amount of creativity and thought that is exchanged freely. There is enormous value in the free exchange of ideas and expression. Second, no creative work is created from nothing. An author of a book owes a lot to all sorts of prior learning, conversations, and prior writing both public domain and copyrighted.

      Of course, many people hold a moderate position. A person who has worked on a parcel should be respected for that work and have privileges over there, but it is not the same as owning an object and should have limits. Personally, I have basically no problem with trademark law, but in practice I feel both copyright and patent law are completely out of whack. In particular, the length of copyright is ridiculously long, and I feel that works like fan fiction and mash-ups should be legal. The implementation of patents seems to mean that most are bogus, and getting a patent is more about knowing how to work the system than inventing something. So while I disapprove of much of blatant pirating, I find it hard to side with the law given my issues with it.
      \n

      \n\nNomdePlume \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Note: if this post gets a little meandering and boring, jump to the dotted line: the section that follows is, I humbly claim, the most important post in this thread.\n
      Posted By: Seth DrebitkoAnother consideration is to have steps involved in the cracking. For example I don't know anyone who pirates software or for the most part video games. The steps after downloading the software to get it to work is enough of a turn off. So really you just need something that is simple to access legally and anoying to pirate instead of un-crackable.
      \nMaybe that was true once, but now pirate copies of software and games are as easy to install as the legal version. In fact, one of the most compelling arguments against DRM is that the pirate copy is often easier to install, and more reliable than the DRM version. Look through the history of any video game discussion forum, and you'll find accounts of people being unable to install games or crashes in games that are traced to the DRM, and are avoided by using a CD-key or other method of bypassing the DRM.
      I have experienced that first hand.
      (Note: It's not that the DRM-creators are incompetent, it's just adding extra complexity provides more points of failure. Plus, that added code is designed to stop the game working under certain circumstances- and no solution is perfect. It's going to 'work' at times it isn't meant to.)

      Here are my experiences with games, that occurred pretty close together in time (while this is a digression, I have a reason for detailing these examples):

      I bought Bioshock from direct2drive.com, which needed to be activated online before I could use it. Every time I came to play it, it told me it needed to be activated, and to their credit, D2D's support was very good. They admitted the DRM in Bioshock was problematic, and after working through all possible solutions, they just gave me an artificially boosted number of activations (instead of the 5 usually allowed). But I still needed to activate each time, so I gave up, and bought the steam version when it was on sale. It didn't put me off Direct2Drive though, since I'd bought other games from them and didn't have this problem.

      I bought Burnout Paradise from EA games, and found it just would not install. I found their technical service atrocious (every time I dealt with a different rep, and they kept asking me the most basic questions - that I'd already given them the answers to in my initial posting of the problem), and the only way I could solve this problem was to download a pirate copy of the game.

      I bought the steam version of Chronicles of Riddick: Escape from Butcher's Bay, and for a time all was well. But then I had a couple of problems with my computer, and needed to reinstall windows a few times, each time installing the game. Then I got a new computer, and tried installing it on that and found I couldn't - I was out of activations. Then I discovered they used a DRM technology called TAGES, and the most onerous implementation of that technology. You are allowed 5 activations, and each change of hardware or computer uses up your activations. So I contacted Steam, they referred me to Atari who make they game. They did not answer at all, but on their forums I found a link to the TAGES site, and contacted them, and they told me they didn't deal with the customer directly, I needed to take it up with Atari. And Atari once again did not reply at all. I did use multiple email addresses to try to contact them, but the consensus on the forums was that they just didn't care, and didn't bother replying - so off to the download sites I went.

      Now, after experiences like this, I don't buy new games anymore - I don't have that much disposable income, so I keep an eye out for sales. One thing I've noticed when re-installing windows, transferring to new computers, etc, those pirate copies require much less maintenance than my legally acquired games - with the exception of those bought through steam. Which is the only example I have encountered of DRM-done-right - DRM that adds value.

      So, because of incidents like these, the game industry has lost money from me. The question is: is the loss they have inflicted worth it, for them? I'll get to that in a moment.

      While writing this I had the observation that the creative industries are actually different to other products a consumer may buy: there is a special risk to a consumer. if I buy a fridge, or a car, or stereo, and it doesn't work, I can return it - maybe sometimes the store will try to object, but in the end they'll accept it. Okay, a bunch of cowboys may do a shoddy job of building my patio, and the legal route to a resolution may not practically be available, but in general, most tangible goods have a legal requirement to be suitable for the purpose they were bought.

      It's a lot fuzzier with creative properties. If I buy a book, or film, or rpg, or software, and the advertising turns out to be misleading, and it turns out to be not what I expected, I can't usually return it. So there is a genuine risk to the consumer, that reading reviews can only mitigate a little - not eliminate entirely. That's why I believe a lot of the people who say they download as a try-before-buy, and then if they like it, they buy it.

      I'm not sure if that point is relevant, but I'm rambling anyway so I just thought I'd throw it out there in an attempt to write a post longer than one of Eero's.

      Still, there are also plenty of people who download who don't fit into that category. And so, lots of other people say, "game X had a piracy rate of 90%, so 90% of gamers are pirate scum and are killing the video game industry." Sadly, the people who make those claims aren't maths geeks. Here's a post from another debate on video game piracy, that was largely ignored by the other debaters, because they each had their own viewpoints and principles to preach, and weren't overly concerned with the facts.

      -------------------------------

      In my experience the vast majority of pirates will pirate almost everything they hear about and buy virtually nothing, ever.

      Consider this thought experiment:

      There is a community of 10,000 gamers, half of them only buy games, half only pirate.

      There is a pool of 100 games for these guys to choose from.

      Each paying customer buys about 5 games per year.

      Each paying customer has a ~5.1% chance of buying your game for an average of ~255 sales.

      We will pretend the unrepentant pirates will pirate half the games out there.

      That is ~2500 pirates for your game.

      Or about 90% of your player base.

      This is all completely unsubstantiated conjecture.

      But it might help put things in perspective.

      Even if every unrepentant pirate would buy games if they could not be bought, that wouldn't mean that you would get 10 times more paying customers, it would mean that at best your would get 2 times with these numbers.

      -------------------------
      Think about the above. Understand it.

      The actual numbers can be juggled in lots of different ways. But what it tells you is: the number of active pirates in a society can be small, but because they are greedy and download everything in sight, it's possible to say things like: "90% of the copies of this game or book or movie in circulation are pirate copies! our industry is doomed!" While at the same time, it can be and probably is true, that the extra customer base available to you, if every pirate was forced to become a consumer, might be quite small.

      Here's an article to a similar point of view, from the people who gave the Indie games Bundle - 5 indie games for as little as you want to pay, including $0.01. He showed how the industry might have as little as 10-20% pirates, and yet have accurateb figures claiming that 80-90% of games are pirate. There's an example where there are reasonably hard statistics too - read the iPhone section.
      http://blog.wolfire.com/2010/05/Another-view-of-game-piracy

      So, this whole discussion has been from the point of view of how serious a problem piracy is, but my question is, is it really? We don't have reliable figures. Even wih massive piracy rates, you might only have an extra 100% possible consumers available to you, or maybe even as little as 10% extra. Is it really that big of a problem?

      *experiences a moment of panic after hitting the Add Your Comments button and the post fails to post - please don't make me type this again... whew, it's still there.*

      \n Hi Eero,
      \n
      \n>>Ryan, you've approached this thing from the viewpoint of social contract, so I'm wondering: how do you find a creator's right to compensation different from a grass mower's right to the same? The bit that's not working for me in your insistence on societal rights to payment is that when I mow your lawn I'm entitled to a payment only if we have a prior contract on it; if I do it without you asking, you are not obligated to pay me despite you deriving benefit from it. Why is it that a creator making something and relinquishing his effective control over his creation (by publishing it) gets to expect a payment?

      \n\nchearns \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2010 \n

      \n
      \n Hi, my name is chearns and I'm a file-sharer.

      I'll limit my text here to RPGs (since what do y'all care about how I deal with movies, music, comics, television programs...). I'll also try and limit it to actual stories from my life.

      Dogs in the Vineyard. I copied it. This was the game that got me back into playing RPGs (even though it was years between reading it and playing it). I, I have no idea how, but I found it on a torrent site and DLed it, read it, and was amazed at how far RPGs had come since I walked away (some five years earlier if one is generous, but more like ten really). A year or two later I would go on to buy the game (in pdf form), because, even though I was broke (I am on year five of being unemployed and currently live off of welfare) I had spoken with Vincent a few times on knife-fight and figured I could eat a little less one week. This was the start of my purchasing games when I can.

      Donjon. Was available free online (on Clinton's website), but was the other game that made me say wow, so I bought it too (pdf).

      In case anyone wonders, I buy pdfs because I find buying books to be morally questionable, at best, and down right wrong at worst. So, make of that what you will Joshua.

      D&D fourth. Downloaded it. Read lots of the player's handbook. Felt it was glorifying racial essentialism and white supremacy (also, the game was way too complex for me). Deleted it. Never bought it, never will. Have absolutely no feelings of guilt whatsoever.

      3:16. Downloaded it. Played it. Then read it. Then asked Gregor where I should buy the pdf to maximize his profits, then bought it from there.

      InSpectres. Downloaded it. Read it. Played it. Bought the pdf. In that order.

      Here's one that goes differently. Downloaded someone's book about how to play RPGs (not going to name it), thought it was brilliant and it most definitely affects how I play RPGs today. The author is a huge bigot though (as far as I can tell, on the issues of gender and sexual orientation), and so I refuse to pay for the pdf.

      Mortal Coil. Downloaded the first edition. Read it. Said to myself, hey, I think this game will interest my partner, so we talked about it, and sure enough, we decided we'd play. However, I'd rather play from the revised pdf as opposed to the first edition plus the errata, so I purchased the revised pdf.

      Microcosm. Free game. I put it on a file sharing site because it's great fun. Interestingly, this is the only game I've ever put on a file-sharing site. Not sure what to make of that.

      Random rpg (this is what usually happens). I download it. And then one of the following happens. I find it racist or sexist, and delete it. There's too much fluff (setting and fiction), so I delete it. The mechanics don't interest me, so I delete it. Almost every download has that happen to it.

      There are a few other stories, games that I've read, that I still have the copied pdf for, haven't played and haven't bought. I feel pretty confident that one day they'll either be deleted or paid for.

      \n
      \n Johnstone,

      I think physical resources belong to the public. So the resources used as materials for the book, the resources that put the book together, the resources that are used to ship the book, and the space required to store the book prior to sale, are put to poor use when the entire product can be shipped without petrol as a series of ones and zeros. Everyone pays when those resources are lost, and yet they are considered to be privately owned. Wood and petrol affect my ability to breathe (as well as everyone else's), they are hardly suitable for private consumption when other options exist.

      When it comes to moral actions, I usually just ask the question, what if everyone did this (that's right, what if six billion people did it). When it comes to resource usage, the answer is usually it would be better if we didn't.

      Obviously, this doesn't apply to people who can't use a pdf. Like, say, someone who doesn't own a computer.

      ******

      Originally I was going to answer in a whisper, thinking it had nothing to do with the conversation at hand, but I realize it might help understand the perspective behind the actions I've taken. I mean, if you can write me off as unreasonable and bizarre for my viewpoint on physical artefacts, then my file-sharing habits too can be written off as unreasonable and bizarre and inapplicable to the real world.

      \n I realize I've been following this thread with interest, yet have not contributed anything but a few funny videos, so here's my personal experience with file sharing.
      \n
      \nI've downloaded music like crazy, using Limewire. Got all those songs I couldn't afford (in some cases, even find), and play them on my computer. I've slowed down my downloading almost to a halt. Like, how much music do I need? Every once in a while, there's a song I want, and I'll go look for it on Limewire.
      \n
      \nI don't often go to concert. When I do (not often), and I like the artist (often), and I can afford it (relative), I'll buy their CD right then and there.
      \n
      \nI have also downloaded a few movies and tv show episodes. And I've watched movies on youtube cut in parts.
      \n
      \nNow, for games and books. So far, I've only downloaded free pdfs of games and books (the latter because they were in the public domain) or I've bought them (often with a book+pdf bundle deal). I do file share games, but only with friends. I don't torrent.
      \n
      \nI don't torrent in part because I never learned how, my previous Internet arrangement had an upload/download limit. My current one doesn't, but the computer I'm using isn't very powerful, uses some version of Linux and I can't seem to figure out how to install anything new on it, and I'm just not very motivated to learn because I'm in a "down with comsumerism!" phase.
      \n
      \nMaybe that'll change in the future.
      \n
      \nIdeologically, I'm all for the free sharing of information, art and ideas. I also believe the creator must be compensated, duh. But I'm a bleeding heart socialist, you know. I don't believe people should have to work to live. I believe everyone should get the minimum they need for food, shelter and dignity, and any money they make with their work should be to supplement that amount. But that's another discussion.

      \n I can't really think of anything Eero has said that I disagree with. I think he expressed my position better than I ever could.

      I also hate physical copies of things that are electronically experienced. The idea of owning a physical disc for music or a movie seems pretty awful to me. I hate clutter and I hate stuff that sits there for the majority of the time.

      It's just so much easier to keep my movie library scattered across hundreds or thousands of computers spread all over the world. And if I like something enough for repeat watching, I can buffer it on my hard drive for immediate access.

      Similarly, I keep my music library scattered across thousands of computers spread all over the world. I also keep some buffered on my hard drive for easy access and for transfer to portable devices.

      I can't wait for eReaders and non-light emitting screens to truly come into their own. I already keep a vast library of eBooks scattered across thousands of computers all over the world. I can definitely see myself accessing that library a lot more once I have a true portable "book" device. We're almost there.

      \n\nPogueMahone \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n edited August 2010
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n First, let me state, because, unfortunately, it seems necessary in internet discussions to do so, that I don't support copyright infringement, and that I support the idea of copyright and moral rights in general.

      Moral copyright is a term I've never seen used in any literature I've ever read, and I suspect is a conflation of two things, 1) the natural law justification for copyright, and 2) moral rights.

      Moral rights are separate and distinct from copyright.

      French moral rights include the following:
      Right of attribution--generally, the right to be recognized as the author of a work
      Right of integrity--the right to prevent the work from being destroyed or mutilated, "or any nonconsensual alteration which deforms, substantially recasts, or misrepresents the creator's conception of the work."
      Right of divulgation--generally, the right to make the work known to the public
      Right of withdrawal and modification--generally, the right to withdraw the work from publication (upon payment of an indemnity)

      Berne article 6bis (adopted at the Rome convention in 1928) only covers the first two, and in compromised language:
      "(1) Independently of the author's economic rights, and even after the transfer of the said rights, the author shall have the right to claim authorship of the work and to object to any distortion, mutilation or other modification of, or other derogatory action in relation to, the said work, which would be prejudicial to his honor or reputation."\n
      Posted By: Adam Dray quoting wikipedia
      The preserving of the integrity of the work bars the work from alteration, distortion, or mutilation. Anything else that may detract from the artist's relationship with the work even after it leaves the artist's possession or ownership may bring these moral rights into play.[emphasis in original]
      \nA standard rule of statutory interpretation is that the general that follows the specific is circumscribed by the specific. So, "other derogatory action" doesn't just mean "anything else," but means anything like the former. See Ejusdem Generis. Every case or article that I've read that deals with the right of integrity usually entails something like Adam's KKK hypo, the destruction of a sculpture, or prints that use slightly different colors than the original artwork. I've never seen the right of integrity used in a case of run-of-the-mill copyright infringement (like Adam's torrenting or FATAL/SenZar bundling hypos). Of course, I'd be perfectly willing to accept that I haven't read every case or article and be pointed to one where this was successfully argued.\n
      Posted By: Adam Dray
      Moral rights do, in fact, place copyright in the category of inalienable human right. This is the basic gist of the Berne Convention, which the vast majority of countries of the world have signed. That is, moral rights are by definition natural (fundamental, inalienable) rights. You may believe otherwise, but the vast majority of the world is on the other side of that argument.
      \nAgain, moral rights are separate and distinct from copyright. Copyright is not inalienable. Inalienable doesn't mean fundamental under natural law, it means that the thing can't be bought, sold, or transferred. Thus, even in France, and in every country that recognizes copyright, copyright is alienable. Moral rights, despite not being expressed as such in Berne, are inalienable in every jurisdiction I'm aware of.

      ---

      OK, so with regard to the hypotheticals:

      Putting a free PDF up as a torrent without the author's consent--copyright infringement; the fact that it is free will affect damages, but it is still copyright infringement. It is copyright that gives the author the exclusive right of distribution.

      Putting a work on the back of a KKK document--copyright infringement and arguably a violation of the author's right of integrity; If the author has the copyright they have a claim. Adam is right that the U.S. provides very limited protection for an author's moral rights. Let's say the author no longer has the copyright, and the copyright owner is unwilling to litigate, or the author actually licensed the work to the person who then without the author's consent published it on the back of a KKK document: in the US, the author could make a claim under 43(a) of the Lanham Act for false endorsement (basically, she could prevail if the court finds that there is a likelihood that the public will think that she endorses the KKK). In France, the author could simply argue a violation of her right of integrity (it'd likely be a much easier argument).

      Ara

      \n Haven't read all 262 posts, so I'm actually just responding to the original post.

      I do download PDFs of games, but it's simply not practical to play off the PDFs - reading from a screen is tiresome and trying to scroll around just doesn't work as easily. If I'm at all going to play a game, it's necessary to buy the physical copy. I figure that's fair enough, if I'm getting enjoyment out of playing it, the writers get some money. If I never play it, well, I've spent hundreds of dollars on books I never got to play with, can't afford to keep doing that.

      My attitude's really the same for anything else - I've got a limited budget so I stick to buying the stuff that's really good, and I usually can only tell that after the fact.

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2010 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Thanks for the clarifications, Ara. I intentionally used the term "moral copyright" to fend off confusion (with "natural rights") that would result if I started talking about "moral rights." I've seen "moral copyright" used in places to specify which part of copyright (the moral rights part) a writer is discussing.

      Most of my hypotheticals were intended to make a point about how I felt I could be harmed by different kinds of acts that may or may not be legal infringement. I don't know if the bundling tort would hold up in civil court, for example, but it'd still bother me.
